B00007J4SI|5.0|When talking about Scriabin, of course, maturity is only a relative term. He died in 1915 at only 43 years old. He composed an extensive large collection of piano music. Scriabin was a very complicated person and his life is surrounded by many myths. He is known to only be able to compose after extreme intoxication or physical intimacy. Scriabin was able to express so many different emotions with his music from anger, fear and mystery to light, love and ecstasy. This 5 CD set allows us to hear his development as a composer. The very first piece on the set, the 3 Pieces op. 2, is one of my favorite pieces, the Etude in C sharp minor. These first 3 pieces were composed when he was only 15 years old. The second, a Prelude, gives a taste of things to come in its extreme beauty that Scriabin was able to capture in only 45 seconds. The third is an Impromptu whose melodic and harmonic style as well as the nature of the left hand accompaniment could almost have been lifted from a page of Chopin. Several of the earlier pieces show the influence of Tchaikovsky, and in the more virtuoso pieces, of Liszt. There could scarcely be a greater contrast between this music and the music from the end of Scriabin's life. From the early 1900's he began to be interested in matters divine and mystic, and as these preoccupations found their way into the music, the music became less immediately accessible, less melodic, and distant from what what others were composing at the time. Scriabin began to view the music he composed in the later years of his life as a sort of preparation for some huge project which would bring together his religious, mystic and musical philosophies. His death intervened, and we can never know how his very particular thinking might have developed, nor to what conclusions, musical or otherwise, he might eventually have been led by it all. The music of this period is characterized by new harmonies, based on the interval of the fourth, and mystic chords became associated with him. Fantasie in B Minor is typical of this period, at one moment somber and sad and then burning with a kind of diabolical energy at another.  One of my favorite pieces of Scriabin's music is the whole set of 24 Preludes, op.11. The Preludes in the black keys are the most interesting, especially Nos.13-18. Hardly two minutes in length, these short works take the pianist through the whole keyboard with cross hands, accentuating chords requiring all ten fingers to run across the black keys with great intensity. No.13, which starts off in a slow manner, is rich with sustained chords in the left hand and gliding arpeggios in the right which just end on a hanging note. The Preludes in some aspects have connections to Chopin's preludes. Scriabin's teacher was a student of Chopin. The influence is subtle but very real. Scriabin's desire to reach new heights surface in works like the Three Morceaux. The first work, Feuillet d album, is extremely slow and looks so playable to an inexperienced pianist. However, it is full of expression that extends beyond just the fingers and a lot of thought and feeling has to be put into playing it. Anyone interested in Scriabin's music definitely needs to get this set performed by Michael Ponti. It contains all of Scriabin's piano works except for the sonatas and a couple of other works that are also available from Vox Box on a 2 CD set.

1400063515|5.0|More good ideas and quotes per page than any other book I've read recently. One of the key concepts reminds me of the geologic argument years ago between uniformitarianism (the processes operating today are the same that operated in the past) and catastrophism. In the Black Swan, Taleb poses the argument that the processes that today are not necessarily the processes that will operate in the future due to \\"unknown unknowns\\" -- highly improbable events that are not only impossible to forecast, but that have definite and very profound effects (drastically changing the future). After the fact we build a story to explain what was not only unexplainable but also inconceivable, as if it had always been (or should have been) obvious. 1416523359|5.0|I never write reviews about the books I read here on amazon because it always seems as though others have written and the consensus would not be changed by my review.  Here, however, I was surprised to find the all the negative reviews.  I greatly enjoyed \\"Lisey's Story\\" for the same reasons others seemed to find it unbearable.  I have read just about everything Stephen King has written.  As a teenager, I enjoyed his earlier stuff because it was gorier and more bloodthirsty.  It was what I required after coming from reading mostly R.L. Stine and Christopher Pike.  Now that I am a bit older, I enjoy his newer stuff because it has heart as well as gore.  This story reminded me of \\"Bag of Bones\\" and gave me the feeling I had at the end of that story--they both deal with the inner as well as the outer.  Yes, the inner language of Lisey and Scott's marriage took time to work into, but I've been around couples before who have their own rich inner language and it makes one feel uneasy not to be part of it.  As soon as I felt part of that inner language (or could at least navigate around it), I enjoyed it.  My husband and I have our own fledgling language which puts the outsider at a disadvantage, so it was great to see something like that portrayed.  This type of inner language was also portrayed in the case of Lisey and her sisters (this includes her parents and grandparents) as well as with Scott and his father and brother (this was another complex relationship that was portrayed beautifully).    I was also surprised by the reaction to Lisey's behaviour and the opinion that this behaviour was not logical or believable.  To say that Lisey has to do what she does because the plot demands it is a ridiculous and belittling statement that could be made of any action that any character has ever taken in any work of literature.  A writer is always working with some kind of plot in mind so, of course, the characters must act in certain ways; it is only a matter of making those actions believable, which I think King does.  Also it is ironic that this charge is brought up at all, since King deals with that odd kind of reaction in his book.  \\"Reality is Ralph,\\" he says and that is exactly the case.  Truth is stranger than fiction as the cliche tells us and literature is supposed to mirror a reality that can be strange and alien.  I found Lisey to be a multi-dimensional character and I could empathize with her loss.  I don't think (as others have alleged) that the introduction of Boo'ya Moon was at all forced, as it had been eluded to several times earlier in the book.  I think it is childish to expect an author to come clean with all his tricks and plot twists at the beginning of the story.  \\"Lisey's Story\\" is complex and it does take work to get through it.  If you are looking for an easy read (something akin to sitting down and watching a show on television where it is not necessary to think), I would suggest a different book.  This is a book where emotions run deep, you experience loss, love, forgiveness, and, yes, horror.  This is a Stephen King book after all.

0374105235|5.0|A Long Way Gone: Memoirs of a Boy Soldier by Ishmael Beah is a moving, tortured yet uplifting story of Beah's involvement in war.<br /><br />Living in Sierra Leone, Beah was a typical 12-year old, playing soccer, dancing, singing rap music, reciting Shakespeare and hanging out with his friends.  Beah and a bunch of his buddies ventured to another town when civil war came to their village.  Not being able to make it back home, they were forced to flee--trying to find somewhere safe as well as a source of food.  Their goal was to avoid being captured or killed by the rebels.  Instead, they were discovered by the government army and turned into soldiers.  Some of these boys were so slight that they couldn't even hold the AK-47s they were given as weapons.  They were also given prodigious amounts of illegal drugs.  For three years, Beah served with the army until UNICEF removed him from military service.  During those three years, he was shot a number of times and escaped death repeatedly.<br /><br />While Beah's physical injuries healed, the psychological scars from the war tortured him for years.  He especially suffered from nightmares and migraines.  With the help of the staff of UNICEF and NGO, he not only healed enough to be \\"repatriated,\\" but he was also chosen as a representative to the United Nations First International Children's Parliament.  It was here that he met the woman who would become his surrogate mother, and arrange for his eventual escape from Sierra Leone.<br /><br />Ishmael Beah is a powerful, eloquent voice for the many children who were forced to become soldiers.  These children were robbed of their families, their limbs, their childhoods, and often, their lives.  After a nightmare, \\"I would try desperately to think about my childhood, but I couldn't.  The war memories had formed a barrier that I had to break in order to think about any moment in my life before the war.\\"<br /><br />I wish that Beah had gone into more detail about his journey to the United States.  Perhaps he's saving it for another book.  But even without this information, A Long Way Gone is an excellent book by a very young author.ps he's saving it for another book.  But even without this information, A Long Way Gone is an excellent book by a very young author.

B000B8IA2G|5.0|For quite a long time, I never liked Stargate; but now, after seeing Atlantis, I realize that I was very wrong. Everything about this show is almost perfect--almost perfect acting, strong storylines, and incredible special effects!    First of all, the actors:    Joe Flanigan is great as John Sheppard; he gives the character a wry twist, somewhat like Jack O'Neill but a bit less ironic. Rainbow Sun Francks makes Aiden Ford likeable and goodnatured, but willing to take charge when needed. Torri Higginson is better at playing Elizabeth Weir than her SG-1 predecessor, although her character isn't very interesting until the 14th episode. Rachel Luttrell is calm, cool and collected as Teyla. And, of course, David Hewlett, as Rodney McKay, is both annoying, endearing, and hilarious! As for supporting actors, some of the best are Paul McGillion as Dr. Carson Beckett, David Nykl as Dr. Radek Zelenka, and Craig Veroni as Dr. Peter Grodin.    Each of the episodes are very well written. SPOILERS FOLLOW for anyone who hasn't seen them!    1: Rising. 10/10. This episode starts off the adventure very well, with Daniel Jackson figuring out the riddle of the Lost City. We get to see the city quietly hiding underwater and, even better, erupting upwards in a blaze of sunlight. Colonel Sumner's militaristic personality makes us appreciate Sheppard even more. We meet the Wraith, run away from the Wraith, and in the end realize that we haven't seen the last of them. It sets the stage for the stories to come.    2: Hide and Seek. 9/10. Very, very interesting. Of course, it's regrettable that the whole problem was started by some foolish kids, but otherwise it's great. The entire scene with Rodney and his personal shield is one of my favorites!    3: Thirty Eight Minutes. 10/10. This has a tense feel to it. Sheppard's strength is ever so slowly being sucked away by a hideous bug, but no one can help him because their 'shuttlecraft', the Puddle Jumper, is stuck in the Stargate! Tensions rise and, surprisingly, the whining Dr. Kavanaugh helps more than he hurts.     4: Suspicion. 7/10. Okay, but not one of my favorites. The constant mistrust of Teyla's people, the Athosians, in season 1 gets to be annoying.     5: Childhood's End. 9/10. This episode is pretty interesting, involving a group of children and some more Ancient meddling. Sheppard's team gets in trouble, but ends up unscathed.    6: Poisoning the Well. 7/10. The biggest problem with this episode is the fact that the Hoffans--a people who are willing to die uselessly in order to keep the Wraith away--are not the only stubborn ones in the Pegasus Galaxy. They totally refuse to find a better way to fight the Wraith, and that is quite frustrating.    7: Underground. 8/10. This episode introduces the Genii, militant people who pretend to be farmers and fool even Teyla. Although not one of the best Genii episodes, it's all-around strong, especially with Colm Meaney giving a great performance as Cowen, leader of the Genii.    8: Home. 9/10. The team finds a gate with a supposedly unlimited source of power and thinks they can use it to go back to the Milky Way, but of course problems arise. Surprisingly, this episode is not quite as predictable as you might think! Near the end you slowly begin to realize just what's going on, but throughout the entire episode, you're fully in the dark.    9: The Storm. 10/10. This is one of the best episodes in the series so far. A giant storm threatens Atlantis--they begin to prepare--but they'll have to fight off the Genii first! Rodney and Dr. Zelenka come up with a great way to temporarily defend the city, and Commander Kolya is introduced. A wonderful episode.     10: The Eye. 10/10. The conclusion to The Storm, this is another stellar episode. We see what happens to the city and its inhabitants, and face the Genii. Thankfully, they didn't give the storyline a weak conclusion.    11: The Defiant One. 10/10. In this episode, we first see the Ancient Defense Satellite that's featured later on. On the planet below, a single Wraith has survived for ten thousand years and silently waits... Very nerve-wracking and, in a way, sometimes annoying (i.e. the Wraith figuring the Puddle Jumper out in seconds). Rodney's line to Sheppard: \\"What are you, Captain Kirk?\\" Great!    12: Hot Zone. 10/10. One of, if not the best, episodes of season 1. A contagion is released inside Atlantis and is almost impossible to stop. We get quite a bit of foolishness from Dr. Weir, however, who refuses to sensibly deal with a situation, making Sheppard eventually have to disobey orders in order to resolve it. Thankfully, that doesn't take away from the adventures in this episode.    13: Sanctuary. 8/10. An interesting concept, but not so great in the end. After a while, the Ancients lose their luster--stupidly refusing to destroy the Wraith and punishing those Ancients who try to. It doesn't help that the entire episode goes by without the Atlantis team gaining any allies or getting a safe haven to evacuate to.    14: Before I Sleep. 10/10. Finally, a Dr. Weir episode that deepens her character! It's too complicated to say much about, but in the end we finally respect and appreciate Elizabeth. A wonderfully complicated time-travel concept helps to add the sci-fi.    15: The Brotherhood. 8/10. Sadly, this episode is just like Sanctuary. It leaves Atlantis with absolutely nothing, all because of Rodney's big mouth. In a way, it's slightly frustrating.    16: Letters from Pegasus. 10/10. This episode is both escapist and intense. Teyla and Sheppard try to rescue some of the former's friends from the Wraith, while everyone else records messages to send to SGC in a quick data-burst. It shows a hidden side of everyone, which helps to deepen their characters drastically.    17: The Gift. 9/10. A secret is revealed, and Teyla becomes even more valuable--in a way. This episode serves to give us more information on her and her people, so it's good to watch.    18: The Siege, Part One. 10/10. A wonderful, tense, horrific, and dazzling episode. The Wraith finally come to Atlantis, despite the efforts of Peter Grodin, an intelligent and likeable British doctor, who's regrettably killed when the Wraith destroy the Ancient Defense Satellite. No one knows if they'll survive, and there seems to be no hope.     19: The Siege, Part Two. 10/10. Just as good as Part One. A group of Marines show up from SGC, bearing somewhat good news, but in the end it seems that even they won't be good enough to stave off the Wraith. It leaves you nervous and very impatient for Part Three!    All in all, Stargate Atlantis is one of the best sci-fi shows I've ever seen. Even if you've never seen SG-1, you should try to get this. You definitely won't be disappointed.
159102594X|5.0|I picked up The Blade Itself with a pile of other fall evening reading.     I began and by ten pages in found myself unwilling to spare the time to eat. Normally one or two characters capture my fancy and I accept the others as necessary to the unfolding of the tale. Here though I found them ... gripping, puzzles full of surprising twists and insights and turns of phrase.     When it ended, I went to my computer and ordered the second volume from England. As soon as it arrived I stopped all else and began Before They Are Hanged. Now that too is done. I long for volume 3.    I do not begrudge simple heroes their time. Those who wallow in self-induldgent angst hold no charm for me. The folk of these tales are are neither. I do not know if I like them but I do know I want more. As much as I can get.
0553805460|1.0|I just finished this book and was really, really disappointed.  The only reason I read it is because it was supposed to be considered as a movie by Martin Scorsese, as it touted on the back of the dust jacket.  Apparently Leonardo DiCaprio would play runt Jordan Belfort, and Terrence Winter (Sopranos) was set to write the screenplay.<br /><br />A blurb in \\"Daily Variety\\" (March 26th, 2007) claimed \\"Like 'Catch Me if you Can', 'Wolf' would be a two-hander with a key part for a second star: Much of the film would hinge on Belfort's relationship with the FBI agents who tried to make him an informant.\\"  There was maybe one paragraph devoted to this.  Instead of a suspensefull Wall Street cat-and-mouse as I expected, The Wolf of Wall Street is simply a diary of debauchary, a one-sided confessional by pathetic Belfort.  He comes off so incredibly dislikable, has nothing good to say about anybody, and in the end rats out all those who were loyal to him just to save his skin.  He claims he did so to keep his gold-digging ex-wife from indictment, but that logic is never explained.  It's so obvious he squealed to save himself.  There's nothing heroic, or interesting, about a rat, especially a drug addict rat.<br /><br />Scorsese/DeCaprio making this book into a film astounds.  He already made \\"The Departed\\" about informants within Boston's Irish mafia.  At least that story had life-or-death consequences.  The last shot is of a rat so that theme has already been explored in his films.<br /><br />With Great Gatsby Leonardo is going to be in back-to-back movies playing rich guys in suits.  How about someone we can relate to Leo?  Take a page from the roles Ben Affleck chooses.<br /><br />Like the thousands of Belfort's victims, the Stratton Oakmont and other investors, I feel cheated (albeit I got off only losing the price of this lame book, plus the time it took me to read it)<br /><br />Ni doubt the publisher will paperback this with release of movie and Belfort will host numerous talk shows, but this should be in the bargain bin.
